reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
"""
Three-stage MLP on packed activations: h1 = relu(h0 @ W1), h2 = relu(h1 @ W2), out = h2 @ W3.
Dimensions are fixed; weights are trainable parameters with default init.
"""

import torch
import torch.nn as nn

from kernelbench.distributed_collectives import default_device

B = 16
H0 = 48
H1 = 64
H2 = 40
H3 = 24


class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        self.W1 = nn.Linear(H0, H1, bias=False)
        self.W2 = nn.Linear(H1, H2, bias=False)
        self.W3 = nn.Linear(H2, H3, bias=False)

    def forward(self, h0: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        h1 = torch.relu(self.W1(h0))
        h2 = torch.relu(self.W2(h1))
        return self.W3(h2)


def get_inputs():
    dev = default_device()
    g = torch.Generator(device=dev)
    g.manual_seed(14)
    return [torch.randn(B, H0, device=dev, generator=g)]


def get_init_inputs():
    return []

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward pass of a three-stage MLP: h1 = relu(h0 @ W1), h2 = relu(h1 @ W2), out = h2 @ W3. B=16, H0=48, H1=64, H2=40, H3=24. All no bias. We need to write a ModelNew class that loads inline CUDA extension via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. We need to provide a host-side wrapper functions in cpp_sources with signatures, and list them in functions=[...]. The wrapper is callable from Python. The kernel should be faster than PyTorch reference. Given sizes are small (B=16, H0=48, H1=64, H2=40, H3=24), the op is basically a sequence of three small GEMMs with ReLU interleaved. The reference uses `nn.Linear` which calls cuBLAS under the hood. For such small sizes, overhead may matter. But perhaps a fused kernel combining all three GEMMs and ReLUs can be faster because it avoids intermediate global memory round-trips and kernel launch overhead. We should write a custom kernel that uses shared memory and possibly register tiling to compute the entire pipeline per block. But we need to match numerically fp32. We can implement a straightforward matrix multiply with manual loops. Since dimensions are fixed and known at compile time, we can pass them as template parameters for maximum optimization. However, with load_inline, we can just use constexpr in the kernel or pass via dimensions. We need to think about GPU specs. Let's get them first.

All operations are matrix multiplications with ReLU activations This is a small MLP. We need to fuse the operations into a single CUDA kernel for speed. Given the dimensions are small: - h0 @ W1: 16x48 @ 48x64 = 16x64 - h1 @ W2: 16x64 @ 64x40 = 16x40 - h2 @ W3: 16x40 @ 40x24 = 16x24 Since batch size is 16 and the dimensions are relatively small, we can write a custom CUDA kernel that fuses all three matmuls and ReLUs together. For small matrices, we can consider: 1. A naive fused kernel where each thread computes one output element 2. Using shared memory for tiling 3. Since B=16 is small, we might even want to have threads cooperate on the same output row Let me think about the compute and memory: - Total flops: 2 * B * (H0*H1 + H1*H2 + H2*H3) = 2 * 16 * (48*64 + 64*40 + 40*24) = 32 * (3072 + 2560 + 960) = 32 * 6592 = 210,944 FLOPs - Total memory for weights: 48*64 + 64*40 + 40*24 = 3072 + 2560 + 960 = 6592 floats = ~26KB - Input: 16*48 = 768 floats = ~3KB - Output: 16*24 = 384 floats = ~1.5KB This is tiny! A single thread block could theoretically handle this. The PyTorch reference will have kernel launch overhead for each matmul. So the key is to fuse everything into one kernel to eliminate kernel launch overhead and avoid intermediate reads/writes to global memory. Let me write a fused kernel. Since the problem is so small, I can: - Load all weights into shared memory or registers - Have each thread compute one or more output elements Actually, with 16 batch size and small hidden dims, we can have each thread block compute the full output for one or more batch elements. With B=16, we'd have 16 blocks. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. We want to maximize occupancy and minimize latency. Let me think about using a warp-level approach. Actually, a simple and effective approach for this small problem: each thread block handles one batch element, and threads within the block compute the output in parallel. Block size: 128 threads - For layer 1: each of 64 threads computes one element of h1 (dot product of 48) - Synchronize - For layer 2: each of 40 threads computes one element of h2 (dot product of 64). The remaining 88 threads are idle. - Synchronize - For layer 3: each of 24 threads computes one element of h3. Remaining threads idle. Actually for something this small, let me consider just loading the weights into registers and having each thread compute everything. Let me design the kernel more carefully: ```cuda __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ W1, const float* __restrict__ W2, const float* __restrict__ W3, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H0, int H1, int H2, int H3) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // One block per batch element if (b >= B) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sh1 = smem; // H1 elements float* sh2 = smem + H1; // H2 elements const float* x = h0 + b * H0; // Layer 1: x @ W1 -> h1 (with ReLU) for (int i = tid; i < H1; i += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < H0; j++) { sum += x[j] * W1[j * H1 + i]; } sh1[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } __syncthreads(); // Layer 2: h1 @ W2 -> h2 (with ReLU) for (int i = tid; i < H2; i += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < H1; j++) { sum += sh1[j] * W2[j * H2 + i]; } sh2[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } __syncthreads(); // Layer 3: h2 @ W3 -> out for (int i = tid; i < H3; i += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < H2; j++) { sum += sh2[j] * W3[j * H3 + i]; } out[b * H3 + i] = sum; } } ``` Wait, the weight layout matters. Wait, actually Ampere SM has 128 FP32 CUDA cores. For FP32, each warp scheduler can dispatch to 32 FP32 cores, so a warp (32 threads) takes 1 cycle. 4 warp schedulers can dispatch 4 warps simultaneously to 128 FP32 cores. On Hopper, it's similar but with Tensor Core improvements. So 128 FP32 cores per SM, 4 warp schedulers. So one SM can do 128 FMAs per cycle. With 2.0 GHz clock, that's 256 GFLOPs per SM. For our kernel: 16 blocks * 6592 FMAs = 105,472 FMAs total. At 256 GFLOPs per SM, one SM would take 105,472 / 256e9 = 0.41 microseconds. With all 16 blocks running on one SM (time slicing), it's still 0.41 microseconds. Spread across 16 SMs, it's 0.025 microseconds per SM, plus overhead. PyTorch likely takes 10-20 microseconds due to kernel launch overhead. So our kernel should be much faster even if not perfectly optimized. Wait, but there's another consideration. And since we want W1_t[j, i] for adjacent i to be adjacent in memory, let me verify: W1[i, j] is at offset i * 48 + j in physical memory. W1_t[j, i] corresponds to W1[i, j], which is at offset i * 48 + j. For fixed j, as i varies, the offset is i * 48 + j. The stride between W1_t[j, i] and W1_t[j, i+1] is 48 floats = 192 bytes. That's NOT adjacent in memory! Oh no! .t() in PyTorch doesn't physically transpose; it just changes strides. So W1_t is still stored with stride 48 between elements in the "new" second dimension. So if I pass W1_t to the kernel and have threads access W1_t[j, i] for fixed j and varying i, the memory access is strided by 48, not coalesced. I have two options: 1. Wait, actually in CUDA, divergence is handled at the warp level. If threads 0..7 and 16..23 take the if path, the warp issues the if-path instruction with those threads active. Since W1 is read-only and cached, the second warp hits L1 cache. This is excellent! The memory access is perfectly coalesced. And there's no inter-warp divergence, only intra-warp. No cross-batch divergence in a warp 3. Two warps run concurrently in a block So the design is: - Block size: 64 threads (2 warps) - Grid size: 8 blocks (for 16 batches) - Warp 0 handles batch 0 of the block, warp 1 handles batch 1 - Each warp uses __syncwarp() for synchronization - No __syncthreads() needed since warps don't share data Wait, they do share shared memory. Shared memory is per-SM, and writes by one warp should be visible to other warps in the same block without sync if they access different banks. But actually, shared memory accesses within a warp are naturally visible to other threads in the same warp without explicit sync for the same thread, but across different instructions, we need __syncwarp to ensure ordering. For our case, each warp writes to shared memory and then reads from it in the next layer. The reads happen in later instructions, and within a warp, the writes are visible to the same warp after completion. But CUDA documentation says memory operations within a warp are not guaranteed to be ordered without __syncwarp(). So we should use __syncwarp(). Let me write the kernel for 64 threads per block, 8 blocks: ```cuda __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ W1, const float* __restrict__ W2, const float* __restrict__ W3, float* __restrict__ out) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int warp_id = tid / 32; // 0 or 1 const int lane_id = tid % 32; // 0..31 const int b = blockIdx.x * 2 + warp_id; // Each warp gets its own shared memory region __shared__ float smem[2 * (64 + 40)]; float* my_smem = smem + warp_id * (64 + 40); float* my_sh1 = my_smem; float* my_sh2 = my_smem + 64; const float* x = h0 + b * 48; // Layer 1: h1 = relu(x @ W1) { const int i0 = lane_id; const int i1 = lane_id + 32; float sum0 = 0.0f; float sum1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 48; j++) { const float xj = x[j]; sum0 += xj * W1[j * 64 + i0]; sum1 += xj * W1[j * 64 + i1]; } my_sh1[i0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0); my_sh1[i1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1); } __syncwarp(); // Layer 2: h2 = relu(h1 @ W2) { const int i0 = lane_id; const int i1 = lane_id + 32; float sum0 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { sum0 += my_sh1[j] * W2[j * 40 + i0]; } my_sh2[i0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0); if (i1 < 40) { float sum1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { sum1 += my_sh1[j] * W2[j * 40 + i1]; } my_sh2[i1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1); } } __syncwarp(); // Layer 3: out = h2 @ W3 { const int i = lane_id; if (i < 24) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 40; j++) { sum += my_sh2[j] * W3[j * 24 + i]; } out[b * 24 + i] = sum; } } } ``` Wait, I realize that __syncwarp() only syncs threads within the same warp, and both warps in the block call __syncwarp(). OK so transposition is necessary for optimal memory access. The question is how to do it efficiently. With `.t().contiguous()`, the allocation is done on the CPU (for the tensor metadata) but the actual memory allocation for the data is on the GPU. But since adjacent threads read rows that are 48 floats apart (192 bytes), each read loads a different cache line. A warp of 32 threads reading W1[i*48+j] for i=0..31 will cause 32 cache line loads (assuming 128B cache lines and each row access is to a different line). That uses 32 * 128B = 4KB of cache bandwidth per inner loop iteration. In contrast, with transposed weights and coalesced access, the warp reads 2 cache lines (for 32 contiguous floats = 128 bytes, which is exactly one cache line? OK, let me write the code now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline B = 16 H0 = 48 H1 = 64 H2 = 40 H3 = 24 cuda_source = """ __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ W1, const float* __restrict__ W2, const float* __restrict__ W3, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int warp_id = tid >> 5; const int lane_id = tid & 31; const int b = (blockIdx.x << 1) + warp_id; if (b >= B) return; __shared__ float smem[2 * (64 + 40)]; float* my_smem = smem + warp_id * (64 + 40); float* my_sh1 = my_smem; float* my_sh2 = my_smem + 64; const float* x = h0 + b * 48; // Layer 1: h1 = relu(x @ W1) { const int i0 = lane_id; const int i1 = lane_id + 32; float sum0 = 0.0f; float sum1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 48; j++) { float xj = x[j]; sum0 += xj * W1[j * 64 + i0]; sum1 += xj * W1[j * 64 + i1]; } my_sh1[i0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0); my_sh1[i1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1); } __syncwarp(); // Layer 2: h2 = relu(h1 @ W2) { const int i0 = lane_id; const int i1 = lane_id + 32; float sum0 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { sum0 += my_sh1[j] * W2[j * 40 + i0]; } my_sh2[i0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0); if (i1 < 40) { float sum1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { sum1 += my_sh1[j] * W2[j * 40 + i1]; } my_sh2[i1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1); } } __syncwarp(); // Layer 3: out = h2 @ W3 { const int i = lane_id; if (i < 24) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 40; j++) { sum += my_sh2[j] * W3[j * 24 + i]; } out[b * 24 + i] = sum; } } } void fused_mlp_forward(at::Tensor h0, at::Tensor W1, at::Tensor W2, at::Tensor W3, at::Tensor out) { int B = h0.size(0); int grid = (B + 1) / 2; fused_mlp_kernel<<<grid, 64>>>( h0.data_ptr<float>(), W1.data_ptr<float>(), W2.data_ptr<float>(), W3.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B ); } """ class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self): super().__init__() self.W1 = nn.Linear(H0, H1, bias=False) self.W2 = nn.Linear(H1, H2, bias=False) self.W3 = nn.Linear(H2, H3, bias=False) def forward(self, h0): # Transpose weights for coalesced access W1_t = self.W1.weight.t().contiguous() W2_t = self.W2.weight.t().contiguous() W3_t = self.W3.weight.t().contiguous() out = torch.empty(B, H3, device=h0.device, dtype=h0.dtype) fused_mlp.fused_mlp_forward(h0, W1_t, W2_t, W3_t, out) return out # Compile at module load time fused_mlp = load_inline( name="fused_mlp", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_mlp_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], ) ``` Wait, there's a problem. `load_inline` might complain if there's no `cpp_sources`. In the kernel, if I access it as `W[j * 64 + i]`, I'm assuming contiguous storage with stride 64. But the actual offset is `j + i * 48`. Mismatch! So I can't just pass the transposed view without `.contiguous()`. I need to pass the correct pointer and strides. If I pass strides to the kernel: ```cuda sum += xj * W[j * W_stride0 + i * W_stride1]; ``` For the transpose view: W_stride0 = 1, W_stride1 = 48. So `W[j * 1 + i * 48]`. For contiguous transpose: W_stride0 = 64, W_stride1 = 1. So `W[j * 64 + i * 1]`. If I pass the original weight without transpose: W_stride0 = 48, W_stride1 = 1. `W[j * 48 + i * 1]`... wait, that's for the original layout where output i, input j is at i*48 + j. But we're computing `sum_j x_j * W[i, j]`. With the original layout, we'd access `W[i * 48 + j]`. Hmm, if I restructure the kernel so that the inner loop is over output i instead of input j... no, that's what we're already doing. Let me be very explicit: - Reference computation for one output: `out_i = sum_{j=0}^{47} x_j * W1[i, j]` - W1 stored row-major: `W1[i, j]` is at physical address `base + i * 48 + j` - Thread computing `out_i` does: `for j: sum += x[j] * W1[i * 48 + j]` If thread 0 computes out_0 and thread 1 computes out_1: - Thread 0 reads W1[0*48 + j], W1[0*48 + j+1], ... - Thread 1 reads W1[1*48 + j], W1[1*48 + j+1], ... At step j, thread 0 reads W1[j], thread 1 reads W1[48+j]. These are 48 floats apart. To get coalescing at step j, I need adjacent threads to read adjacent memory. So I need thread i to read W1[something + i]. In the transposed approach where W1_t[j, i] = W1[i, j] and W1_t is stored contiguously (shape 48x64, stride 64x1): - `out_i = sum_j x_j * W1_t[j, i]` - W1_t[j, i] is at `base + j * 64 + i` - Thread i reads W1_t[j * 64 + i] - At step j, threads 0..31 read W1_t[j*64 + 0..31]. Adjacent! Coalesced! This is the correct approach. And to make W1_t contiguous without runtime cost, I can do it in __init__. The benchmark probably doesn't check parameter shapes or expect parameters to change after initialization. Let me modify to pre-transpose: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self): super().__init__() self.W1 = nn.Linear(H0, H1, bias=False) self.W2 = nn.Linear(H1, H2, bias=False) self.W3 = nn.Linear(H2, H3, bias=False) # Pre-transpose and make contiguous for efficient kernel access self.register_buffer('W1_t', self.W1.weight.data.t().contiguous()) self.register_buffer('W2_t', self.W2.weight.data.t().contiguous()) self.register_buffer('W3_t', self.W3.weight.data.t().contiguous()) def forward(self, h0): out = torch.empty(B, H3, device=h0.device, dtype=h0.dtype) fused_mlp.fused_mlp_forward(h0, self.W1_t, self.W2_t, self.W3_t, out) return out ``` Wait, but `self.W1_t` won't be updated if `self.W1.weight` is updated during training. For inference, it's fine. But does the benchmark check training? The problem says "forward computation", so it's likely just inference. If the weights are fixed after initialization, this is fine. To be safe, I should do the transpose in forward. It's only 3 small tensor views and contiguous calls. But let me check the PyTorch overhead. `t()` is instantaneous (view creation). `contiguous()` copies data if not contiguous. For transposed linear weights, it always copies. For 12KB + 10KB + 4KB = 26KB, the copy is at ~1TB/s (device bandwidth for small copies might be lower). 26KB / 500GB/s = 52 nanoseconds. That's essentially nothing. Actually, the overhead is more in the Python function call and kernel launch. The actual memory copy is done by a CUDA memcpy kernel, which takes some microseconds for setup. But for 26KB, it's probably 1-2 microseconds. If the total kernel time is 3-5 microseconds, an extra 2 microseconds is 50% overhead. That's significant! Wait, `contiguous()` might not launch a kernel. For small contiguous allocations on CUDA, PyTorch might use a synchronous copy or a very small async kernel. Let me not worry too much and see. Actually, thinking about it more, PyTorch's `Tensor.contiguous()` for a transpose on GPU does launch a `copy_` kernel or uses the memory coalescing engine. For very small tensors, the fixed overhead might be 5-10 microseconds. If that overhead is too much, our kernel might not be faster than the reference! The reference also has overhead: PyTorch's matmul launches kernels, and the weights are passed in their original layout. PyTorch's matmul might internally transpose the weights (cublas often handles transposition internally). So actually, cublas might transpose the weights inside the matmul kernel! If so, PyTorch also pays the transpose cost, but it's done within the matmul kernel efficiently using shared memory. If our kernel has to transpose outside, we have an extra overhead that PyTorch doesn't have (because PyTorch's kernel fuses the transpose into the matmul using shared memory tiling). This is a critical point! For small matrices, cublas/ CUTLASS kernels use shared memory to load and transpose tiles of the weight matrix on the fly. So the reference doesn't have a separate transpose step. Our kernel would be faster if it also handles the original layout efficiently but avoids the multiple kernel launches. Or we need to accept that the transpose cost is small. To match PyTorch's efficiency, I could write a kernel that doesn't require transposed weights but still achieves good shared memory behavior. For small GEMV (batch=16, input=48, output=64), a typical efficient approach is: - Each warp handles one sample - Threads in the warp load a tile of input and a tile of weights into shared memory - Use warp-level reduction to accumulate But with H0=48, one warp can load all 48 inputs into shared memory. And for weights, since each output is a dot product of the 48 inputs with a row of W1, the warp can compute all 64 outputs by having threads compute partial sums and reduce. Actually, for GEMV with a small input vector, the most efficient approach is: 1. Load all 48 inputs into shared memory (broadcast-like, all threads load the same values). 2. For each output, have all 32 threads compute a partial dot product over 48/32 = 1.5 elements each, then warp-reduce. 3. With 64 outputs, do this 64 times. For 64 outputs and 32 threads: - Each output is computed by all 32 threads doing partial sums. - Thread tid handles elements j = tid and j = tid + 32 (2 elements each, but 48 means some handle 1). - After computing the partial sum, use `__shfl_down_sync` to sum across the warp. - Each warp takes 64 iterations to compute 64 outputs. Each iteration: load 2 weights (one for each of 32 threads, but the weights depend on output i), multiply by inputs from shared memory, warp reduce, store result. But the weight loading is: for output i and input j, load W1[i, j]. At iteration i, thread 0 reads W1[i, 0], thread 1 reads W1[i, 1], etc. And then for j = tid + 32, thread 0 reads W1[i, 32]. So the weight loads for the first part (j=tid) are coalesced! Threads 0..31 read W1[i, 0..31], which is a contiguous 32-float chunk. Perfect coalescing! For the second part (j=tid+32), threads 0..31 read W1[i, 32..63]. Wait, W1 has shape (64, 48), so j only goes from 0 to 47. For j = tid + 32, only j = 32..47 are valid. So threads 0..15 read W1[i, 32..47] and threads 16..31 are idle (or don't participate). These 16 threads read a contiguous 16-float chunk. Also coalesced! This is amazing! By parallelizing the reduction dimension (j) instead of the output dimension (i), we get perfect coalescing for weight loads! And we don't need to transpose the weights at all! Let me work out the algorithm: For each batch element b (handled by one warp): 1. Load all 48 inputs x[0..47] into shared memory (or registers). Since all threads need all inputs, we can have the warp load them collaboratively: - Threads 0..31 load x[0..31]. Coalesced. - Threads 0..15 load x[32..47]. Coalesced. - Store in shared memory. - __syncwarp() 2. For each output i = 0..63: - Initialize partial sum for each thread. - For j = tid where tid < 48: sum += x_smem[j] * W1[i * 48 + tid] - For j = tid + 32 where tid + 32 < 48 (i.e., tid < 16): sum += x_smem[j] * W1[i * 48 + tid + 32] - Warp reduce the 32 partial sums to get the final sum for output i. - Apply ReLU and store in smem_h1[i]. Wait, but warp reduction requires all 32 threads to participate. If we only have 48 inputs and 32 threads: - Thread tid computes partial sum for j = tid (if tid < 48) and j = tid + 32 (if tid + 32 < 48, i.e., tid < 16). - Actually, we can divide 48 inputs among 32 threads as: - Threads 0..15: 2 inputs each (j and j+32) - Threads 16..31: 1 input each (j) Then we warp reduce all 32 partial sums. Since threads 16..31 only have 1 term, their partial sum is just the product. Threads 0..15 have 2 terms. For the warp reduction, all 32 threads participate. The `__shfl_down_sync` can sum across all threads. Implementation of warp reduce: ```cuda __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } return val; } ``` Then for each output i: ```cuda float partial = 0.0f; if (lane_id < 48) { partial += x_smem[lane_id] * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; } if (lane_id < 16) { partial += x_smem[lane_id + 32] * W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) { h1_smem[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } ``` Wait, `warp_reduce_sum` gives the total sum to all 32 threads (or just thread 0 if we don't broadcast). Actually, `__shfl_down_sync` moves data from thread lane+offset to thread lane, so after the reduction, thread 0 has the total sum. If only thread 0 writes, the other threads are idle for the write. We could instead use shared memory for h1 and have thread 0 write each output. But then thread 0 is the bottleneck. Another option: after reduction, broadcast the sum to all threads, and each thread writes its assigned output. But we'd need to iterate over outputs. Actually, for 64 outputs, if we have 1 warp with 32 threads, and each output requires a warp reduction, we'd need to launch 64 warp reductions. Each warp reduction takes about 5 shuffle instructions. 64 * 5 = 320 instructions per warp. Plus the loads. Total about 500-600 instructions. Not bad. But this is for one batch element. For 16 batch elements, we'd need 16 warps (or 16 blocks of 1 warp each). Wait, in this approach, one warp computes ALL 64 outputs for one batch element. The loop over 64 outputs is sequential within the warp, but the inner dot product is parallel across the warp. For 64 outputs: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { float partial = 0.0f; if (lane_id < 48) partial += x[lane_id] * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 16) partial += x[lane_id + 32] * W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]; float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) h1[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } ``` And then similar for layer 2 and 3. For layer 2: 40 outputs, each dot product of 64 elements. - Threads 0..31: load h1[j] where j = lane_id. All 32 threads have one element. - Threads 0..31: also load h1[j+32] where j = lane_id + 32. But h1 only has 64 elements. So threads 0..31 would handle j and j+32. - For each output i = 0..39: - partial += h1[lane_id] * W2[i * 64 + lane_id] (all 32 threads) - partial += h1[lane_id + 32] * W2[i * 64 + lane_id + 32] (all 32 threads, lane_id+32 < 64) - warp reduce - if lane_id == 0: h2[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum) For layer 3: 24 outputs, each dot product of 40 elements. - Threads 0..31: load h2[j] where j = lane_id. Only j < 40 are valid, so threads 0..39 would participate. But we only have 32 threads. - Threads 0..31: handle j = lane_id (32 values) - Threads 0..7: also handle j = lane_id + 32 (8 values) - For each output i = 0..23: - partial += h2[lane_id] * W3[i * 40 + lane_id] (if lane_id < 40, all 32 threads) - partial += h2[lane_id + 32] * W3[i * 40 + lane_id + 32] (if lane_id < 8) - warp reduce - if lane_id == 0: out[b * 24 + i] = sum Wait, for layer 3, lane_id ranges from 0..31. The condition `lane_id < 40` is always true. So all 32 threads participate in the first partial product. For the second partial product, only lane_id < 8 (i.e., lane_id < 8 since 40-32=8) participate. So all 32 threads are always active in the first partial product, and 8 threads are active in the second. Excellent utilization! And the best part: W1[i * 48 + lane_id] for lane_id=0..31 is a contiguous 32-float chunk. Coalesced! W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32] for lane_id=0..31, but only lane_id<16 is active (since j only goes up to 47). For layer 1, threads 0..15 read W1[i*48 + 32..47]. Also coalesced! For layer 2: W2[i * 64 + lane_id] for lane_id=0..31 is a contiguous 32-float chunk. And W2[i * 64 + lane_id + 32] for lane_id=0..31 is another contiguous 32-float chunk. All 32 threads participate. Perfect coalescing! For layer 3: W3[i * 40 + lane_id] for lane_id=0..31 is a contiguous 32-float chunk. W3[i * 40 + lane_id + 32] for lane_id=0..7 is a contiguous 8-float chunk. Coalesced! This is beautiful! And we don't need any weight transposition! But there's one issue: for layer 1, W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32] with lane_id < 16. This means lanes 0..15 read W1[i*48 + 32..47]. But in the first partial product, ALL lanes 0..31 read W1[i*48 + 0..31]. So the warp does: 1. All lanes read W1[i*48 + lane_id] (coalesced, 128 bytes for 32 floats) 2. Lanes 0..15 read W1[i*48 + 32 + lane_id] (coalesced, 64 bytes for 16 floats) Wait, in the `if (lane_id < 16)` block, only 16 threads are active. The `__shfl_down_sync` reduction needs all threads to participate. So we compute the first partial product with all 32 threads, then the second partial product with 16 threads (divergence), then warp reduce. This is similar to our previous kernel but in reverse: we're doing the divergence in the dot product accumulation rather than the output assignment. Actually, comparing the two approaches: - Approach 1 (output-per-thread): no transpose needed if we accept strided access (or we transpose weights). With transposed weights, all threads are busy most of the time, and memory is coalesced. But we pay transpose overhead. - Approach 2 (reduction-per-warp): no transpose needed. All threads are busy most of the time. Memory is coalesced. No transpose overhead. But we have loop overhead for the 64/40/24 outputs. Approach 2 seems better! Let me estimate the instruction count: - Layer 1: 64 outputs * (2 loads + 2 FMAs + warp_reduce_5_shuffles) = 64 * (2 + 2 + 5) = 576 instructions per warp - Layer 2: 40 outputs * (2 loads + 2 FMAs + 5 shuffles) = 360 instructions - Layer 3: 24 outputs * (2 loads + 2 FMAs + 5 shuffles) = 216 instructions Total: ~1152 instructions per warp In Approach 1: - Layer 1: 48 iterations * (1 load x + 2 loads W + 2 FMAs) = 48 * 5 = 240 instructions for the main loop, plus some overhead. Times 2 for 2 outputs per thread = 480 instructions. Wait, Approach 1 has each warp handling 2 batch elements with 32 threads each. Let me recalculate for 1 batch per warp: - Layer 1: each thread does 48 iterations with 2 FMAs. 48 * 2 = 96 FMAs + 48 loads x + 96 loads W = 240 instructions. Plus __syncwarp. Times 32 threads, but SIMT executes in parallel. - Actually in SIMT, the warp executes 48 iterations, each with 32 threads doing 2 FMAs. So the instruction count is about 48 * (load + 2 FMA + sync overhead) = maybe 200 warp instructions. - Layer 2: ~64 warp instructions * number of active paths - Layer 3: ~40 warp instructions Approach 1 has fewer total instructions because it doesn't do warp shuffles. But Approach 1 requires weight transposition. Given that Approach 2 avoids the transpose entirely, it might be faster overall. But there's a memory bandwidth consideration. In Approach 2, each warp reads: - Layer 1: 64 outputs * 48 floats = 3072 floats from W1 = 12KB per warp. 16 warps = 192KB total. - Layer 2: 40 outputs * 64 floats = 2560 floats = 10KB per warp. 16 warps = 160KB total. - Layer 3: 24 outputs * 40 floats = 960 floats = 4KB per warp. 16 warps = 64KB total. These weight reads happen from global memory (cached in L1/L2). Since the total weight size is 26KB and all warps read it, the L2 cache handles this well. After the first few warps, the weights are in L2. In Approach 1 with transposed weights, the same amount of weight data is read, but in a coalesced pattern. The actual bandwidth is similar. For Approach 2, the weight access pattern in layer 1: - For each output i, all 32 threads read W1[i*48 + 0..31] and then W1[i*48 + 32..47]. - Since output i changes with each loop iteration, this is a streaming access pattern across the weight matrix. - W1 is 64x48 = 3072 floats = 12KB. It fits in L1 cache (256KB per SM). So most reads hit L1 cache after the first pass. For 64 outputs, the warp iterates over all 64 rows of W1. Since W1 is 12KB and L1 is 256KB, it fits easily. After the first few outputs, the entire W1 is in L1 cache. Subsequent reads are L1 hits, very fast. Similarly for W2 (10KB) and W3 (4KB). So Approach 2 should be memory-latency-bound rather than bandwidth-bound, and having many warps helps hide latency. But we only have 16 warps total. Actually, in Approach 2, each warp does a lot of work (computing all outputs for one batch element). 16 warps for 16 batch elements. The SMs can run multiple warps concurrently if there are enough registers. With Approach 1 (2 warps per block, 8 blocks), that's also 16 warps total. Same warp count. So both approaches have similar parallelism. Approach 2 avoids the transpose and has a very regular memory access pattern. Let me go with Approach 2. Wait, but Approach 2 has 64 iterations in layer 1 per warp, which means 64 `__shfl_down_sync` reductions. Each reduction is ~5 instructions. 64 * 5 = 320 shuffle instructions. Shuffle instructions have some latency (a few cycles). But with no dependency between iterations (except the loop counter), the compiler can pipeline them. Actually, each iteration depends on the previous one finishing if we use the same registers. But we can unroll the loop and use different registers. Full unrolling of 64 iterations would require 64 sets of registers for the partial sums and outputs. That's too many. But we can unroll by a factor of 2 or 4 to get some ILP. Wait, we can process multiple outputs per iteration of the outer loop by having each thread compute partial sums for multiple outputs simultaneously. For example, unroll by 2: ```cuda #pragma unroll 2 for (int i = 0; i < 64; i += 2) { float p0 = 0, p1 = 0; if (lane_id < 48) { float xj = x[lane_id]; p0 = xj * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; p1 = xj * W1[(i+1) * 48 + lane_id]; } if (lane_id < 16) { float xj = x[lane_id + 32]; p0 += xj * W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]; p1 += xj * W1[(i+1) * 48 + lane_id + 32]; } float s0 = warp_reduce_sum(p0); float s1 = warp_reduce_sum(p1); if (lane_id == 0) { h1[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, s0); h1[i+1] = fmaxf(0.0f, s1); } } ``` This computes 2 outputs per unrolled iteration, with independent partial sums p0 and p1. This helps hide shuffle latency because while one shuffle is in flight, the next can be computed. Well, not exactly, because s0 and s1 are computed sequentially due to warp_reduce_sum dependence. But the compiler might interleave instructions. Actually, we can process 2 outputs with warp reduction for both, but the reductions can't overlap within the same warp because they use the same shuffle network. One reduction must finish before the next starts (or they can be pipelined if we have enough independent instructions between shuffle stages). Alternatively, we can have half the warp (16 threads) reduce for output i while the other half reduces for output i+1. But warp shuffle works across all 32 lanes, so we'd need custom lane masking, which is complex. For simplicity, let me just not unroll aggressively or use `#pragma unroll 2` to get some benefit. Actually wait, there's a much simpler and more efficient approach for Approach 2: use shared memory for the weights and load them in a coalesced way, then do a parallel reduction in shared memory. For layer 1: 1. Load all 48 inputs into shared memory. 2. For output i, the 32 threads load 32 weights W1[i, 0..31] into registers, multiply by x[0..31], write to shared memory partial sum array. 3. Then threads 0..15 load W1[i, 32..47], multiply by x[32..47], add to shared memory. 4. Then do a parallel reduction in shared memory for the 32 partial sums. But step 2 requires writing 32 floats to shared memory, then reading them back for reduction. That's 64 shared memory operations per output. The shuffle approach is faster because it uses registers. Let me stick with shuffle reduction. Actually, I just realized I can make Approach 2 much faster by batching the warp reduction. Instead of computing one output at a time, I can compute partial products for multiple outputs simultaneously using vectorized accumulators. But given the small problem size, let me first implement the simple version and measure. OK, I'll implement Approach 2 (no transpose, warp-level reductions). Let me write the kernel. ```cuda __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ W1, const float* __restrict__ W2, const float* __restrict__ W3, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int warp_id = tid >> 5; const int lane_id = tid & 31; const int b = (blockIdx.x << 1) + warp_id; if (b >= B) return; __shared__ float smem[2 * (64 + 40)]; float* my_smem = smem + warp_id * (64 + 40); float* my_h1 = my_smem; float* my_h2 = my_smem + 64; const float* x = h0 + b * 48; // Load inputs into registers (all threads need all inputs) // Actually, we can load on demand from global memory since x is small // But loading into shared memory helps for reuse __shared__ float x_smem[2 * 48]; float* my_x = x_smem + warp_id * 48; // Coalesced load of x if (lane_id < 32) my_x[lane_id] = x[lane_id]; if (lane_id < 16) my_x[lane_id + 32] = x[lane_id + 32]; __syncwarp(); // Layer 1: h1 = relu(x @ W1), W1 is (64, 48) #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { float partial = 0.0f; float xj0 = my_x[lane_id]; partial += xj0 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 16) { float xj1 = my_x[lane_id + 32]; partial += xj1 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) { my_h1[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } } __syncwarp(); // Layer 2: h2 = relu(h1 @ W2), W2 is (40, 64) #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = 0; i < 40; i++) { float partial = 0.0f; partial += my_h1[lane_id] * W2[i * 64 + lane_id]; partial += my_h1[lane_id + 32] * W2[i * 64 + lane_id + 32]; float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) { my_h2[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } } __syncwarp(); // Layer 3: out = h2 @ W3, W3 is (24, 40) #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = 0; i < 24; i++) { float partial = 0.0f; partial += my_h2[lane_id] * W3[i * 40 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 8) { partial += my_h2[lane_id + 32] * W3[i * 40 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) { out[b * 24 + i] = sum; } } } ``` Wait, there's a problem. In layer 3, `lane_id` goes from 0 to 31. `my_h2[lane_id]` for lane_id >= 40 would be out of bounds! my_h2 only has 40 elements. For layer 3: W3 is (24, 40). For output i, the dot product is over j = 0..39. - Thread lane_id should handle j = lane_id. But lane_id can be 0..31. For lane_id >= 40... wait, lane_id only goes to 31, which is < 40. So ALL 32 threads can access my_h2[lane_id] without bounds checking! lane_id ranges from 0 to 31, and my_h2 has 40 elements. So my_h2[0..31] is valid. Wait, but `my_h2[lane_id + 32]` for lane_id = 0..7 accesses my_h2[32..39]. my_h2 has 40 elements (indices 0..39). Yes, valid. Valid for lane_id < 16. Perfect! All bounds are correct! But wait, in layer 1, I have: ```cuda float xj0 = my_x[lane_id]; partial += xj0 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; ``` For lane_id = 33, my_x[33] and W1[i*48 + 33] are valid! Now, regarding the `__syncwarp()` after loading x_smem. Since all threads in the warp write to x_smem and then all threads read from it, we need `__syncwarp()`. Then after computing h1, threads write to my_h1 and then all threads read from it. We need `__syncwarp()`. Similarly after computing h2. In the loops, after each iteration, only lane_id == 0 writes. Other threads don't write. So no sync needed between iterations. The writes by lane 0 should be visible to other lanes by the time they read in the next layer (after `__syncwarp()`). But wait, within the loop, `my_h1[i]` is written by lane 0, and other lanes don't read it until the next layer (after the loop and `__syncwarp()`). So within the loop, no sync is needed between iterations. Actually, there is one subtle issue. The `__syncwarp()` after loading x_smem loads x_smem into the warp's execution. In fact, the `__shfl_down` reduction tree computes a prefix-like sum where lane `i` gets the sum of lanes that can reach `i` through the binary tree of offsets going downward. Specifically, lane `i` gets the sum of lanes in the range `[i, i + 2^k - 1]` that align with the tree structure? No, that's not quite right either. Actually, for a standard tree reduction using `__shfl_down_sync`, after all offsets, lane 0 gets the sum of ALL lanes. Lane `i` gets the sum of lanes in the set `{i | (i & (2^k - 1)) == (lane & (2^k - 1)) for all k}`... this is getting complicated. The standard way to do a full warp reduction with broadcast to all lanes is: ```cuda __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); return val; // Only lane 0 has the full sum } ``` To broadcast the result to all lanes, we can use `__shfl_sync`: ```cuda __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); return __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 0); } ``` With `__shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 0)`, all lanes read the value from lane 0. So all lanes get the full sum. But in our kernel, we only need the sum in lane 0 (to write to shared memory). So we don't need to broadcast. We just use the value returned by lane 0. Let me write the final kernel for Approach 2: ```cuda __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ W1, const float* __restrict__ W2, const float* __restrict__ W3, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { const int lane_id = threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; __shared__ float smem[64 + 40]; float* sh1 = smem; float* sh2 = smem + 64; const float* x = h0 + b * 48; // Pre-load input values float x0 = x[lane_id]; float x1 = 0.0f; if (lane_id < 16) { x1 = x[lane_id + 32]; } // Layer 1: h1 = relu(x @ W1), W1 shape (64, 48) #pragma unroll 8 for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { float partial = x0 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 16) { partial += x1 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) { sh1[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } } __syncwarp(); // Layer 2: h2 = relu(h1 @ W2), W2 shape (40, 64) float h1_0 = sh1[lane_id]; float h1_1 = sh1[lane_id + 32]; #pragma unroll 8 for (int i = 0; i < 40; i++) { float partial = h1_0 * W2[i * 64 + lane_id]; partial += h1_1 * W2[i * 64 + lane_id + 32]; float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) { sh2[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } } __syncwarp(); // Layer 3: out = h2 @ W3, W3 shape (24, 40) float h2_0 = sh2[lane_id]; float h2_1 = 0.0f; if (lane_id < 8) { h2_1 = sh2[lane_id + 32]; } #pragma unroll 8 for (int i = 0; i < 24; i++) { float partial = h2_0 * W3[i * 40 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 8) { partial += h2_1 * W3[i * 40 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (lane_id == 0) { out[b * 24 + i] = sum; } } } ``` Wait, the unroll factor of 8 might not help much with shuffles because each shuffle depends on the previous one within the same warp_reduction. But across different output iterations, the loops are independent. The compiler might not be able to fully pipeline them because the warp_reduce_sum uses the same registers each iteration. Actually, we can manually unroll by 2 or 4 to increase ILP: ```cuda #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = 0; i < 64; i += 2) { float partial0 = x0 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; float partial1 = x0 * W1[(i+1) * 48 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 16) { partial0 += x1 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]; partial1 += x1 * W1[(i+1) * 48 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0); float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1); if (lane_id == 0) { sh1[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0); sh1[i+1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1); } } ``` Now partial0 and partial1 are independent! The compiler can interleave the loads and FMAs for both. And while the warp_reduce_sum for partial0 is happening (5 shuffles), some of the setup for partial1 might already be in flight? Not exactly, because warp_reduce_sum is a function call. But with inlining, the compiler can unroll the shuffles and interleave them. With block size = 32 (1 warp), grid size = B = 16. Total warps = 16. Each warp handles one batch element. This maximizes the number of blocks (16), which spreads work across SMs. And each block is a single warp, so no inter-warp synchronization needed. Could I do better with more threads per block? And the two warps in a block might run concurrently on the same SM, improving utilization. But they need separate shared memory regions. And since they don't communicate, having them in the same block doesn't harm. I'll use 32 threads per block for simplicity. If the profiler shows block launch overhead, I can increase to 64. Now, about `__syncwarp()`: in a block of 32 threads (1 warp), `__syncwarp()` is sufficient. After the loop for layer 1, we need to ensure all sh1 writes are visible to other threads in the warp. Since lane 0 writes to sh1[0..63] and other lanes read from sh1 in layer 2, we need `__syncwarp()`. And after layer 2, `__syncwarp()` before layer 3. And after layer 1's x loading, do we need sync? All threads load x[lane_id] from global memory. Since they just read from global memory and the values are lane-private (stored in registers), no sync is needed. But wait, all threads load from `x`, which is global memory. The loads are independent. No shared memory is involved. So no `__syncwarp()` needed for x loading. Let me finalize the code. I need to include the torch extension and define ModelNew. One more optimization: use `float` for everything, no half precision. Let me also check if I need to handle `h0` being non-contiguous. The benchmark likely provides contiguous tensors. I'll assume they're contiguous. For `out`, I allocate with `torch.empty`. I should make sure it's the right size: (B, H3). Here's the final Python code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline B = 16 H0 = 48 H1 = 64 H2 = 40 H3 = 24 cuda_source = """ __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ W1, const float* __restrict__ W2, const float* __restrict__ W3, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { const int lane_id = threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; __shared__ float smem[64 + 40]; float* sh1 = smem; float* sh2 = smem + 64; const float* x = h0 + b * 48; float x0 = x[lane_id]; float x1 = 0.0f; if (lane_id < 16) { x1 = x[lane_id + 32]; } #pragma unroll 2 for (int i = 0; i < 64; i += 2) { float partial0 = x0 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id]; float partial1 = x0 * W1[(i + 1) * 48 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 16) { partial0 += x1 * W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]; partial1 += x1 * W1[(i + 1) * 48 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0); float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1); if (lane_id == 0) { sh1[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0); sh1[i + 1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1); } } __syncwarp(); float h1_0 = sh1[lane_id]; float h1_1 = sh1[lane_id + 32]; #pragma unroll 2 for (int i = 0; i < 40; i += 2) { float partial0 = h1_0 * W2[i * 64 + lane_id]; float partial1 = h1_0 * W2[(i + 1) * 64 + lane_id]; partial0 += h1_1 * W2[i * 64 + lane_id + 32]; partial1 += h1_1 * W2[(i + 1) * 64 + lane_id + 32]; float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0); float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1); if (lane_id == 0) { sh2[i] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0); sh2[i + 1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1); } } __syncwarp(); float h2_0 = sh2[lane_id]; float h2_1 = 0.0f; if (lane_id < 8) { h2_1 = sh2[lane_id + 32]; } #pragma unroll 2 for (int i = 0; i < 24; i += 2) { float partial0 = h2_0 * W3[i * 40 + lane_id]; float partial1 = h2_0 * W3[(i + 1) * 40 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 8) { partial0 += h2_1 * W3[i * 40 + lane_id + 32]; partial1 += h2_1 * W3[(i + 1) * 40 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0); float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1); if (lane_id == 0) { out[b * 24 + i] = sum0; out[b * 24 + i + 1] = sum1; } } } void fused_mlp_forward(at::Tensor h0, at::Tensor W1, at::Tensor W2, at::Tensor W3, at::Tensor out) { int B = h0.size(0); fused_mlp_kernel<<<B, 32>>>( h0.data_ptr<float>(), W1.data_ptr<float>(), W2.data_ptr<float>(), W3.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B ); } """ fused_mlp = load_inline( name="fused_mlp", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_mlp_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self): super().__init__() self.W1 = nn.Linear(H0, H1, bias=False) self.W2 = nn.Linear(H1, H2, bias=False) self.W3 = nn.Linear(H2, H3, bias=False) def forward(self, h0: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: out = torch.empty(B, H3, device=h0.device, dtype=h0.dtype) fused_mlp.fused_mlp_forward(h0, self.W1.weight, self.W2.weight, self.W3.weight, out) return out ``` Wait, in Approach 2, I'm passing the ORIGINAL weights to the kernel. The weights are not transposed. The kernel accesses them in their original row-major layout. And the access pattern is coalesced because the warp loads one column at a time (via the reduction over lanes). In layer 1: `W1[i * 48 + lane_id]`. For fixed i, threads 0..31 read W1[i, 0..31]. Coalesced! And `W1[i * 48 + lane_id + 32]`. For lane_id < 16, these read W1[i, 32..47]. Coalesced! So we pass the original weights. No transpose at all! The reference computes `h0 @ W1.weight.T`. Our kernel computes the same thing but with a different algorithm. Numerical results should be equivalent. Wait, but there's a precision difference. Reference uses whatever algorithm PyTorch/cublas uses. Our kernel uses sequential accumulation with warp reduction. The accumulation order is different: - Our kernel: each lane computes `x[j] * W1[i,j]` for 1 or 2 j values, then we sum across the warp using shuffles. For layer 1, the reference computes each output as a dot product of 48 elements. Our kernel splits the 48 elements among 32 threads (each handling 1 or 2 elements) and sums them with shuffles. The result should be the same mathematically (up to floating point rounding). Since we sum 32 partial products using shuffles, the order is: - First, pair sums: lane_i + lane_{i^16} - Then pair of pairs: lane_i + lane_{i^8} - etc. This is a tree reduction. The order is: - (0+16), (1+17), ..., (15+31) - Then ((0+16)+(8+24)), etc. - Eventually all 32 values are summed. The exact order is different from sequential accumulation. But with 32 values, the difference in floating point rounding is usually small (on the order of machine epsilon times the magnitude, so ~1e-7 for fp32). The tolerance is 1e-4, so we should be fine. For layers 2 and 3, similar tree reductions are used. I'm confident in the numerical accuracy. One final check: does the reference use bias? No, `bias=False` for all three linears. So no bias terms. Are there any other operations? No, just the three matmuls with ReLU in between. My kernel computes: - h1 = relu(x @ W1) - h2 = relu(h1 @ W2) - out = h2 @ W3 This matches the reference exactly. Let me compile and test this. I'll call compile_kernel first. Wait, I notice that I need to call `fused_mlp_forward` after compilation. The `load_inline` call is at module level, so compilation happens when the module is imported. This is fine. But `compile_kernel` only checks syntax. I'll submit the code to it. One more thing: the forward function signature in ModelNew should match `Model.forward(self, h0: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor`. Mine does. And the constructor should match `Model.__init__(self)`. Mine does. Great. Let me compile.

If warp 0's thread t computes output o = t, then for input j, it reads W1[o, j] = W1[t, j]. Thread 0 reads W1[0,j], thread 1 reads W1[1,j]... These are strided by j, not consecutive. No coalescing. To get coalescing with row-major storage, adjacent threads need to read adjacent memory. So adjacent threads should read W1[o, j], W1[o, j+1] for the same o. Or W1[o, j], W1[o+1, j] if j is large and we want to hit the same cache line. Actually, at j=0, W1[0,0] and W1[1,0] are 192 bytes apart. But W1[0,0] and W1[0,1] are 4 bytes apart. For layer 3 output k: out[k] = sum_j W3[k,j] * relu(sum_i W2[j,i] * relu(sum_m W1[i,m] * h0[m])) This is a sum of products of sums of products of sums. If we expand it fully: out[k] = sum_j W3[k,j] * relu(sum_i W2[j,i] * relu(sum_m W1[i,m] * h0[m])) For fixed k, we can compute this by iterating over j, i, m. Wait, but __shfl_xor works on 32 lanes. For 16 lanes, we'd need a different reduction. Actually, for 16 lanes, we can do: ```cpp #pragma unroll for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } ``` This reduces across 16 lanes (lanes 0-15, or 16-31, etc.) if we mask properly. But actually, `__shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset)` with offset=8 swaps lanes 0↔8, 1↔9, ..., 15↔23. That includes cross-subgroup lanes if we only wanted 0-15. To do a subgroup reduction, we need: lanes 0-15 reduce among themselves, lanes 16-31 reduce among themselves. For 16-lane reduction using 32 active threads, we'd need to double the values somehow. Actually, if we just have lanes 0-15 compute one set and lanes 16-31 compute another set, the shuffle with offset=16 would swap between groups. With offset=8, within group. ```cpp // Lanes 0-15: val0, lanes 16-31: val1 val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 8); // 0↔8, 1↔9, ..., 23↔31 // Now lanes 0 has sum of 0 and 8, lane 1 has 1 and 9, etc. val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 4); val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 2); val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 1); // After this, lane 0 has sum of lanes 0,8,4,12,2,10,6,14 // But lane 0 doesn't have lanes 1,3,5,7,9,11,13,15! ``` The standard warp reduction pattern assumes all 32 lanes are reducing the same output. If we have 2 outputs with 16 lanes each, the pattern needs adjustment. For 16-lane reduction, we'd do: ```cpp val += __shfl_xor_sync(0x0000FFFF, val, 8); // Only within lower 16 lanes ``` But the mask `0x0000FFFF` only ensures the lower 16 lanes participate. Actually, `__shfl_xor_sync` doesn't have this flexibility for subgroup-only operations with arbitrary partitioning. Kernel structure: ```cpp __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(...) { int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; // 0 or 1 int b = blockIdx.x; __shared__ float smem[64 + 40]; float* sh1 = smem; float* sh2 = smem + 64; const float* x = h0 + b * 48; // Each warp computes a subset of layer 1 outputs // Warp 0: outputs 0-31, Warp 1: outputs 32-63 int out_start = warp_id * 32; float x0 = x[lane_id]; float x1 = 0; if (lane_id < 16) x1 = x[lane_id + 32]; for (int o = 0; o < 32; o += 2) { int global_o0 = out_start + o; int global_o1 = out_start + o + 1; float partial0 = x0 * W1[global_o0 * 48 + lane_id]; float partial1 = x0 * W1[global_o1 * 48 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 16) { partial0 += x1 * W1[global_o0 * 48 + lane_id + 32]; partial1 += x1 * W1[global_o1 * 48 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0); float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1); if (lane_id == 0) { sh1[global_o0] = fmaxf(0, sum0); sh1[global_o1] = fmaxf(0, sum1); } } __syncthreads(); // Layer 2: Warp 0 outputs 0-19, Warp 1 outputs 20-39 // Wait, 40 outputs, 2 warps. 20 outputs per warp. // 20/2 = 10 iterations of 2 outputs. int out_start2 = warp_id * 20; float h1_0 = sh1[lane_id]; float h1_1 = sh1[lane_id + 32]; for (int o = 0; o < 20; o += 2) { int global_o0 = out_start2 + o; int global_o1 = out_start2 + o + 1; float partial0 = h1_0 * W2[global_o0 * 64 + lane_id]; float partial1 = h1_0 * W2[global_o1 * 64 + lane_id]; partial0 += h1_1 * W2[global_o0 * 64 + lane_id + 32]; partial1 += h1_1 * W2[global_o1 * 64 + lane_id + 32]; float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0); float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1); if (lane_id == 0) { sh2[global_o0] = fmaxf(0, sum0); sh2[global_o1] = fmaxf(0, sum1); } } __syncthreads(); // Layer 3: 24 outputs, 2 warps. 12 outputs per warp. int out_start3 = warp_id * 12; float h2_0 = sh2[lane_id]; float h2_1 = 0; if (lane_id < 8) h2_1 = sh2[lane_id + 32]; for (int o = 0; o < 12; o += 2) { int global_o0 = out_start3 + o; int global_o1 = out_start3 + o + 1; float partial0 = h2_0 * W3[global_o0 * 40 + lane_id]; float partial1 = h2_0 * W3[global_o1 * 40 + lane_id]; if (lane_id < 8) { partial0 += h2_1 * W3[global_o0 * 40 + lane_id + 32]; partial1 += h2_1 * W3[global_o1 * 40 + lane_id + 32]; } float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0); float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1); if (lane_id == 0) { out[b * 24 + global_o0] = sum0; out[b * 24 + global_o1] = sum1; } } } ``` This should be much faster because: - Layer 1: 2 warps each do 16 iterations, total time = 16 iterations (vs 32 in original) - Layer 2: 2 warps each do 10 iterations, total time = 10 iterations (vs 20 in original) - Layer 3: 2 warps each do 6 iterations, total time = 6 iterations (vs 12 in original) Total iterations: 32 (original) vs 16 (new). In CUDA, __syncthreads inside conditional code can deadlock if not all threads in the block reach it. In my kernel, __syncthreads is called unconditionally (it's outside the loops, after them). But within the loops, only lane 0 writes to shared memory. After the loop, all threads must reach __syncthreads. In my kernel, both warps execute the same loop with the same iteration count, so they all reach __syncthreads at the same program counter. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\nB = 16\nH0 = 48\nH1 = 64\nH2 = 40\nH3 = 24\n\ncpp_sources = \"\"\"\nvoid fused_mlp_forward(at::Tensor h0, at::Tensor W1, at::Tensor W2, at::Tensor W3, at::Tensor out);\n\"\"\"\n\ncuda_source = \"\"\"\n__inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0,\n const float* __restrict__ W1,\n const float* __restrict__ W2,\n const float* __restrict__ W3,\n float* __restrict__ out,\n int B) {\n const int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31;\n const int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5;\n const int b = blockIdx.x;\n \n if (b >= B) return;\n \n __shared__ float smem[64 + 40];\n float* sh1 = smem;\n float* sh2 = smem + 64;\n \n const float* x = h0 + b * 48;\n \n // Layer 1: 64 outputs, 2 warps per batch element\n // Warp 0: outputs 0-31, Warp 1: outputs 32-63\n const int out_start1 = warp_id * 32;\n \n float x0 = x[lane_id];\n float x1 = (lane_id < 16) ? x[lane_id + 32] : 0.0f;\n \n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int o = 0; o < 32; o += 2) {\n int global_o0 = out_start1 + o;\n int global_o1 = out_start1 + o + 1;\n \n float partial0 = x0 * W1[global_o0 * 48 + lane_id];\n float partial1 = x0 * W1[global_o1 * 48 + lane_id];\n if (lane_id < 16) {\n partial0 += x1 * W1[global_o0 * 48 + lane_id + 32];\n partial1 += x1 * W1[global_o1 * 48 + lane_id + 32];\n }\n float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0);\n float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1);\n if (lane_id == 0) {\n sh1[global_o0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0);\n sh1[global_o1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1);\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // Layer 2: 40 outputs, 2 warps per batch element\n // Warp 0: outputs 0-19, Warp 1: outputs 20-39\n const int out_start2 = warp_id * 20;\n \n float h1_0 = sh1[lane_id];\n float h1_1 = sh1[lane_id + 32];\n \n #pragma unroll 5\n for (int o = 0; o < 20; o += 2) {\n int global_o0 = out_start2 + o;\n int global_o1 = out_start2 + o + 1;\n \n float partial0 = h1_0 * W2[global_o0 * 64 + lane_id];\n float partial1 = h1_0 * W2[global_o1 * 64 + lane_id];\n partial0 += h1_1 * W2[global_o0 * 64 + lane_id + 32];\n partial1 += h1_1 * W2[global_o1 * 64 + lane_id + 32];\n float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0);\n float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1);\n if (lane_id == 0) {\n sh2[global_o0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0);\n sh2[global_o1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1);\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // Layer 3: 24 outputs, 2 warps per batch element\n // Warp 0: outputs 0-11, Warp 1: outputs 12-23\n const int out_start3 = warp_id * 12;\n \n float h2_0 = sh2[lane_id];\n float h2_1 = (lane_id < 8) ? import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

B = 16
H0 = 48
H1 = 64
H2 = 40
H3 = 24

cpp_sources = """
void fused_mlp_forward(at::Tensor h0, at::Tensor W1, at::Tensor W2, at::Tensor W3, at::Tensor out);
"""

cuda_source = """
__inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) {
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
        val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset);
    }
    return val;
}

__global__ void fused_mlp_kernel(const float* __restrict__ h0,
                                  const float* __restrict__ W1,
                                  const float* __restrict__ W2,
                                  const float* __restrict__ W3,
                                  float* __restrict__ out,
                                  int B) {
    const int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31;
    const int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5;
    const int b = blockIdx.x;
    
    if (b >= B) return;
    
    __shared__ float smem[64 + 40];
    float* sh1 = smem;
    float* sh2 = smem + 64;
    
    const float* x = h0 + b * 48;
    
    // Layer 1: 64 outputs, 2 warps per batch element
    // Warp 0: outputs 0-31, Warp 1: outputs 32-63
    const int out_start1 = warp_id * 32;
    
    float x0 = x[lane_id];
    float x1 = (lane_id < 16) ? x[lane_id + 32] : 0.0f;
    
    #pragma unroll 8
    for (int o = 0; o < 32; o += 2) {
        int global_o0 = out_start1 + o;
        int global_o1 = out_start1 + o + 1;
        
        float partial0 = x0 * W1[global_o0 * 48 + lane_id];
        float partial1 = x0 * W1[global_o1 * 48 + lane_id];
        if (lane_id < 16) {
            partial0 += x1 * W1[global_o0 * 48 + lane_id + 32];
            partial1 += x1 * W1[global_o1 * 48 + lane_id + 32];
        }
        float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0);
        float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1);
        if (lane_id == 0) {
            sh1[global_o0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0);
            sh1[global_o1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1);
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    // Layer 2: 40 outputs, 2 warps per batch element
    // Warp 0: outputs 0-19, Warp 1: outputs 20-39
    const int out_start2 = warp_id * 20;
    
    float h1_0 = sh1[lane_id];
    float h1_1 = sh1[lane_id + 32];
    
    #pragma unroll 5
    for (int o = 0; o < 20; o += 2) {
        int global_o0 = out_start2 + o;
        int global_o1 = out_start2 + o + 1;
        
        float partial0 = h1_0 * W2[global_o0 * 64 + lane_id];
        float partial1 = h1_0 * W2[global_o1 * 64 + lane_id];
        partial0 += h1_1 * W2[global_o0 * 64 + lane_id + 32];
        partial1 += h1_1 * W2[global_o1 * 64 + lane_id + 32];
        float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0);
        float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1);
        if (lane_id == 0) {
            sh2[global_o0] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum0);
            sh2[global_o1] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum1);
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    // Layer 3: 24 outputs, 2 warps per batch element
    // Warp 0: outputs 0-11, Warp 1: outputs 12-23
    const int out_start3 = warp_id * 12;
    
    float h2_0 = sh2[lane_id];
    float h2_1 = (lane_id < 8) ? sh2[lane_id + 32] : 0.0f;
    
    #pragma unroll 3
    for (int o = 0; o < 12; o += 2) {
        int global_o0 = out_start3 + o;
        int global_o1 = out_start3 + o + 1;
        
        float partial0 = h2_0 * W3[global_o0 * 40 + lane_id];
        float partial1 = h2_0 * W3[global_o1 * 40 + lane_id];
        if (lane_id < 8) {
            partial0 += h2_1 * W3[global_o0 * 40 + lane_id + 32];
            partial1 += h2_1 * W3[global_o1 * 40 + lane_id + 32];
        }
        float sum0 = warp_reduce_sum(partial0);
        float sum1 = warp_reduce_sum(partial1);
        if (lane_id == 0) {
            out[b * 24 + global_o0] = sum0;
            out[b * 24 + global_o1] = sum1;
        }
    }
}

void fused_mlp_forward(at::Tensor h0, at::Tensor W1, at::Tensor W2, at::Tensor W3, at::Tensor out) {
    int B = h0.size(0);
    fused_mlp_kernel<<<B, 64>>>(
        h0.data_ptr<float>(),
        W1.data_ptr<float>(),
        W2.data_ptr<float>(),
        W3.data_ptr<float>(),
        out.data_ptr<float>(),
        B
    );
}
"""

fused_mlp = load_inline(
    name="fused_mlp",
    cpp_sources=cpp_sources,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["fused_mlp_forward"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        self.W1 = nn.Linear(H0, H1, bias=False)
        self.W2 = nn.Linear(H1, H2, bias=False)
        self.W3 = nn.Linear(H2, H3, bias=False)

    def forward(self, h0: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        out = torch.empty(B, H3, device=h0.device, dtype=h0.dtype)
        fused_mlp.fused_mlp_forward(h0, self.W1.weight, self.W2.weight, self.W3.weight, out)
        return out
